The train was packed.  
More packed than she thought was even physically possible.

Jen clung to a hanging strap above her head —  
one foot half on, half off the floor —  
and turned wide eyes up at Jeff, who simply stood there,  
stoic, like a brick wall, holding onto the ceiling rail without a care in the world.

**JEN:** (whispering, overwhelmed)  
This is insane.

Jeff grinned, leaned in, and kissed the top of her head casually.

**JEFF:** (smirking)  
Welcome to Tokyo, baby.

The train jerked into motion —  
smooth but forceful —  
and Jen yelped, pitching forward straight into Jeff’s chest.

He caught her easily, strong arms steady,  
pressing her close into the safest, warmest place she could imagine.

She huffed out a breath against his flannel.

**JEN:** (muffled)  
I’m never complaining about rush hour in Cincinnati again.

Jeff chuckled low in his throat,  
pulling her a little closer,  
protecting her from the crush without even thinking about it.

**JEFF:** (whispering)  
You’re doing great.  
  
Half a dozen stops,  
then freedom.

Jen clung to him,  
grinning into his shirt,  
feeling more alive —  
and more theirs —  
than ever before.

---

By the time they surfaced back into daylight,  
Jen felt like she’d been spun through a washing machine.

The fresh air —  
cool, crisp, full of city noise and distant smells of grilled food —  
hit her like a blessing.

She staggered a step sideways, laughing breathlessly,  
clutching Jeff’s arm like she might never let go.

**JEN:** (gasping, laughing)  
We survived.  
  
We survived!

Jeff chuckled low and proud,  
giving her a little squeeze as they crossed into a busy plaza bustling with early workers.

**JEFF:** (grinning)  
Not bad for your first Tokyo rush hour, Mrs. Cremeans.

Jen adjusted her shoulder bag, still catching her breath.

They moved smoothly through the crowd now —  
Jeff leading with the easy confidence of someone who knew where he was going,  
Jen shadowing close behind, still a little dazed but grinning wide.

She glanced up at him, mischievous, playful,  
but a little serious underneath it all.

**JEN:** (light, teasing)  
So, um…  
full disclosure?

Jeff raised an eyebrow, amused.

**JEFF:** (teasing)  
Uh oh.  
That’s never how a good sentence starts.

Jen laughed, nudging him with her hip as they waited at a crosswalk.

**JEN:** (mock sheepish)  
I have no idea how Japanese business meetings are supposed to work.

Jeff just smirked and gave her a lazy shrug.

**JEFF:** (grinning)  
You’ll be fine.  
Smile. Nod. Look interested.

Jen flushed, laughing harder.

**JEN:** (quickly)  
Because honestly?  
  
Everything I know about Japanese business meetings…  
I learned from the movie Rising Sun.

Jeff barked a laugh, loud enough that a few suited businessmen glanced their way.

**JEFF:** (mock horror)  
Rising Sun?  
  
That’s your entire knowledge base?

Jen gave him a scandalous little smile, lifting her chin proudly.

**JEN:** (teasing, sing-song)  
Maaaybe.

She leaned closer, lowering her voice into his ear, wickedly soft:

**JEN:** (whispering)  
And, for the record?  
  
I wouldn’t mind if you ripped my panties off and took me on a boardroom table.

Jeff went absolutely still, feeling a heat crawl up the back of his neck —  
and somewhere lower, too.

Jen pulled back, grinning, her eyes sparkling.

**JEN:** (mock serious)  
Buuut…  
  
I figure that might set the wrong tone for a first meeting.

Jeff shook his head slowly, utterly wrecked and delighted by her at the same time.

**JEFF:** (dry, murmuring)  
You're gonna be the death of me.

**JEN:** (sweetly, brightly)  
You say that like it's a bad thing.

The crosswalk light blinked green,  
and Jeff laced his fingers through hers —  
tight, solid, grounding —  
as they stepped forward into the morning crowds together,  
toward Sony, toward the meeting,  
toward whatever insane adventure came next.

---

The building that housed Sony Japan Music rose sleek and gleaming into the morning sky —  all sharp lines and polished glass,  reflecting the organized chaos of Tokyo back at itself.

Jen felt Jeff’s hand squeeze hers gently once —  one last, wordless exchange —  
before he released her and straightened up, rolling his shoulders back,  his face shifting from her husband to something else. Something… focused.

The playful boyishness from the subway was gone.  In its place was a man fully at work. Jen adjusted the strap of her bag, heart hammering a little harder,  and fell into step half a pace behind him, letting him lead the way through the vast glass doors into the lobby.

The interior was hushed, cavernous, almost clinical —  glossy white marble floors, chrome accents,  occasional soft music filtering down like mist from overhead speakers.

At the reception desk, a young woman sat impeccably dressed,  her posture perfect, her face neutral. Jeff approached without hesitation,  his steps measured, confident but not aggressive.

He stopped at the desk, bowed respectfully at the waist —  low enough to signal seriousness,  but brief enough to mark him as an American familiar with Japanese custom,  but not pretending to be Japanese.

**JEFF:** (calm, professional)  
Good morning.  Jeffrey Cremeans.  I have a 10 a.m. appointment with Mr. Hayakawa.

He produced a simple, crisp business card from a slim leather holder,  presenting it with both hands —  the way he had been taught —  and held it carefully between his thumbs and forefingers as he offered it.

The receptionist accepted it with a polite bow of her own,  handling it with both hands —  
an exchange performed with quiet precision, almost ritualistic.

Jen, standing silently at Jeff’s side, watched with growing awe. There was no fumbling.  
No hesitation.

Jeff stood there as solid as an oak,  his posture respectful but unyielding,  his expression calm and faintly warm —  professional diplomacy, perfectly executed.

The receptionist scanned the card briefly,  then offered a serene, practiced smile.

**Receptionist**: (soft, polite)  
One moment, please.

She picked up the phone and spoke rapid, melodic Japanese into the receiver.

Jeff stepped back half a pace, standing at ease, hands loosely clasped behind his back,  
giving no sign of impatience. Jen stared at him from the corner of her eye, marveling. 
This was not the goofy, teasing man who tried to steal bites of sushi from her plate the night before.  This was something else entirely.

**JEN:** (thinking, quietly stunned)  
This is Jeff at work.

After a few minutes, the receptionist bowed slightly again and spoke.

**Receptionist:** (smiling)  
Mr. Hayakawa will be down shortly to greet you.  Thank you for waiting.

Jeff bowed again — precise, clean, a little deeper this time —  and stepped aside, motioning subtly for Jen to come stand closer to him.

He didn't say a word, but his hand brushed lightly against the small of her back —  
not guiding, not claiming —  just reassuring.

He was telling her, without speaking:  You're fine. You're supposed to be here.

They waited in silence, the vast lobby murmuring with the hum of elevators and muted conversations. And Jen realized something else, standing there at his side —  
something she hadn’t even fully understood until right now: Jeff didn't posture.  
He didn’t pretend to be bigger, louder, or flashier than he was.  He simply was.
Solid. Grounded. Steady. And even here —  on the other side of the world, in a place where he was the outsider —  he commanded a quiet respect.

---

The soft chime of the elevator doors opening echoed across the marble lobby.

Jen turned slightly, heart hammering, as a small group of men emerged —  
five in total —  
moving with the precision of a practiced team.

At the center was Mr. Hayakawa himself —  
short, neatly groomed, a man radiating controlled authority.  
He wore Japanese business casual:  
dress shirt, dark slacks, no tie, no jacket —  
just like the others trailing him.

Their clothing was almost a uniform.  
Muted colors. Minimal flair.

Jen caught a whiff of tobacco smoke as they approached —  
not strong, just a faint, lingering scent,  
like old paper and something older still.

Jeff stepped forward exactly two paces,  
bowed properly —  
a clean, respectful movement —  
and smiled in that calm, grounded way Jen was beginning to realize was pure work mode Jeff.

**MR. HAYAKAWA** (warmly, formal)  
Jeffrey-san.  
  
Good to see you again.

**JEFF:** (even, courteous)  
Mr. Hayakawa.  
  
The pleasure is mine.

They shook hands —  
not too firmly, not too limp —  
and exchanged brief, respectful bows again.

Jen kept her hands loosely folded in front of her,  
standing half a pace behind Jeff —  
exactly where she felt she should be, without even needing to be told.

Then came the moment.

Mr. Hayakawa’s gaze slid toward her —  
measured, appraising —  
then back to Jeff with the faintest flicker of confusion.

**MR. HAYAKAWA** (smiling politely, but pointed)  
And this…Who is this, Jeffrey-san?  Why did you bring her to a business meeting?

There was no malice in the tone —  just a quiet, cutting bewilderment, a deep assumption built into the words: this was a serious space, and serious spaces were not for women.

Especially not foreign women.

Jen felt her stomach twist slightly —  not in shame, but in sudden cold understanding.

Jeff didn’t flinch.  Didn’t bristle.  Didn’t even blink.  He smiled —  small, cool, perfectly controlled.

And then —  still smiling —  he straightened up to his full height, suddenly seeming larger than the room around him.

**JEFF:** (even, firm)  
This is my wife, Jennifer Cremeans.

There was a beat of stillness.  Jen swore the very air went taut between them.

Jeff's voice remained perfectly polite —  but something steely threaded through it now.

**JEFF:** (measured)  
Where I come from…a man does not exclude his wife from his table.  Especially when the table is meant for building trust.

There was no anger.  No defensiveness.  Just an iron truth laid gently on the surface.  Mr. Hayakawa’s expression didn’t change, but something shifted almost imperceptibly in the air, like a ripple passing over still water.

Jen watched it happen, watched the unspoken recalibration, the recognition that Jeff was not a boy to be indulged, but a man who had brought his honor into the room with him.

Slowly, Mr. Hayakawa inclined his head.

**MR. HAYAKAWA** (softly)  
Of course.  Welcome, Jennifer-san.

Jen bowed carefully —  lower than she had seen Jeff do —  and murmured her thanks in English, unsure if he would catch the words but certain he would understand the respect.  The moment passed —  the ground re-leveled itself —  and Mr. Hayakawa gestured with a small, gracious motion.

**MR. HAYAKAWA** (polite)  
Please.  This way.

The entourage peeled off like a school of fish,  and Jen and Jeff followed — side by side now —  moving forward into the polished halls of Sony Japan Music, carrying with them the invisible weight of a line newly drawn and deeply understood.

The group began to move forward —  Mr. Hayakawa’s assistants stepping slightly ahead,  subtle movements of bodies creating a corridor.  Jen instinctively started to follow Jeff —  but one of the junior men, smiling politely, shifted to gently block her path with a shallow, almost imperceptible bow.

A wordless signal:  Not you.

A chill shot through Jen.  She froze, heart pounding. Jeff saw it immediately.

The smile faded from his face,  his body language tightening, not with anger, but with an iciness Jen had never seen before. Jeff didn’t say anything.  Didn’t argue nor posture. Instead, with terrifying finality, he turned slightly toward Mr. Hayakawa, his face composed, his blue eyes flat and cold as steel.

Jeff reached out, calmly but unmistakably, and took Jen’s hand in his own. He held it firmly.  Publicly.  Deliberately. And with the same quiet, nuclear finality, he said two words:

**JEFF:** (even, polite, deadly)  
Good morning.

No anger.  No shouting.  Just two syllables —  like the crack of a hammer splitting granite. Then he turned on his heel —  still holding Jen’s hand —  and walked away.

Walked out of the immaculate marble lobby,  out through the gleaming glass doors, out into the bright Tokyo morning. The autographed LP still under his free arm.  The gift they would not be receiving. He didn’t look back.  Didn’t explain.  Didn’t apologize.

Let them watch his back as he left.  Let them understand, in silence,  exactly what they had done —  and exactly what they had lost.

Jen stumbled slightly to keep up,  struggling to process the quiet, controlled violence of it all. But her hand clung to his —  tight, fierce —  and she realized her heart wasn’t pounding from fear. It was pounding from pride. He hadn’t hesitated. Not for money.  
Not for fame.  Not for the biggest market in Asia. He had picked her —  without a second’s thought —  and chosen honor over profit.

The city roared around them as they moved down the steps and into the street, and Jen finally managed to find her voice.

**JEN:** (soft, overwhelmed)  
Jeff…  You didn’t have to—

He squeezed her hand gently but firmly.

**JEFF:** (quiet, certain)  
Yes.  I did.

He didn’t stop walking.  Didn’t slow down. Just kept moving forward —  into the shining, unfamiliar city —  with his wife at his side,  and his honor absolutely intact.

Jeff hustled.  Didn’t flinch.  Didn’t even tighten his grip on her hand —  just kept moving forward with deliberate, relentless strides. Jen almost had to trot to keep up.

Across the street.  Down the block.  Toward the nearest subway entrance like a man with a mission. He didn't look around.  He didn’t breathe a word.  He just moved. Straight into the bustling crowds,  down the broad concrete steps into the underground.

**JEN:** (thinking, heart pounding)  
Oh my God. He’s furious.

Not on the surface.  Not in any way a stranger would notice. But she could feel it pouring off him —  cold, focused, unstoppable. They didn’t even pause to check a map.  Jeff knew where he was going —  the way a river knows how to find the sea. Through the turnstiles, weaving through crowds,  his jaw set tight, his blue flannel a bold flag cutting through the muted grays and blacks of Tokyo businessmen.

Jen gripped his hand tightly, matching his pace without question. This wasn’t anger over a missed deal.  This wasn’t about money.  This wasn’t even about business. This was about respect. Jeff had worn blue —  his signal, his code —  the same way he always did when something mattered.

They knew.  They knew.

They knew he was American.  They knew he was married.  They knew he was on his honeymoon.  And still, they had chosen to shame her —  to shame him —  to shame themselves. Jeff marched like a man carrying a battlefield in his chest,  and he didn’t slow down until they stood together on the platform,  waiting for the next train. Still silent. Still utterly in command.

Jen stood at his side,  blinking hard against the pressure building behind her eyes. Not fear.  Not sadness. Pride. Absolute, roaring, dizzying pride. Because this man —  this quiet, stubborn, good man —  had chosen her. Over a multi-million-dollar contract.  
Over the prestige.  Over everything.

He had picked her, without blinking.

And somehow,  somehow,  she loved him even more.

---

The subway car was packed again —  crushed shoulder to shoulder —  the low hum of conversation and the mechanical rattle of the train filling the air. But between Jeff and Jen, there was nothing but silence.  Heavy silence. Jeff stood solidly, gripping the overhead rail with one hand,  his other hand loosely, stubbornly holding Jen's.

His jaw was tight.  His eyes fixed somewhere far beyond the slick walls of the subway car.  Focused.  Gone inward. He didn’t fidget.  Didn’t mutter.  Didn’t so much as shift his weight.

But Jen could feel him burning. A low, deep, furious simmer. It rolled off him in waves,  quiet but relentless,  like the invisible tension before a thunderstorm breaks the sky wide open. She squeezed his hand once —  a soft, wordless I'm here — but he didn’t squeeze back yet. He wasn’t ignoring her.  He was containing himself.

The train jolted and rocked as they sped through the neon veins of Tokyo,  and Jen just clung to him —  a lifeline in the shifting crush of bodies. She didn’t try to speak.  It would have been pointless anyway.  Too loud.  Too chaotic. And this —  this wasn’t a moment for words.

This was a man protecting what mattered to him —  at a cost, maybe a great one —  and now carrying the full weight of that choice on his shoulders.

Stop after stop blurred past.  They changed lines once, quickly, efficiently,  still without a word. Jeff moved like a machine —  sure, silent, unstoppable —  and Jen followed, never letting go of his hand. By the time they climbed the long, crowded escalator back to street level near the hotel,  the light outside had changed. Still morning, but the angle sharper now —  everything brighter, harsher, clearer.

---

They climbed out of the subway station,  the roar of the underground fading behind them, the noise of Tokyo rising up again in its place. Jeff moved like a machine — relentless, silent, tight.

But Jen could see it —  feel it —  how close he was to cracking apart inside. The sun was sharp overhead, catching on metal, glass, leaves. Across the street, tucked between two tall buildings,  Jen spotted a small park — nothing fancy, just a sliver of green in the steel and concrete. A few cherry trees framed a narrow path,  branches just beginning to bud,  and a handful of benches sat empty in the late morning light.

Without asking, without explaining,  she tugged his hand gently, insistently.

**JEN:** (firm, low)  
Come with me.

Jeff blinked, disoriented for a half-second,  then followed —  because when she used that voice,  he didn’t argue. She led him across the street,  through the little gate, down the path. And without letting go of his hand,  she sat down heavily on a weathered wooden bench under the nearest cherry tree.

Jeff hesitated —  standing stiffly, the autographed LP still tucked awkwardly under his arm —  like he couldn’t quite figure out what to do with himself. Jen tugged his hand again.

**JEN:** (soft, commanding)  
Sit.

Slowly, heavily, he obeyed —  dropping down beside her,  his body still thrumming with unspent adrenaline. Jen turned sideways on the bench, facing him directly,  their knees brushing lightly.  She waited until he finally looked at her —  until those brown eyes, raw and furious and brokenhearted,  met hers. And then she spoke —  quiet, but unshakable.

**JEN:** (steady)  
Talk to me.  Unburden yourself, Jeffrey Lee Cremeans.

Jeff closed his eyes for a moment,  his breath hitching once. When he opened them again,  they were bright and glassy. And finally —  finally —  he spoke.

---

Jeff sat there for a long moment,  his hands resting uselessly on his knees,  the LP still trapped awkwardly between them. The wind stirred the branches above,  loosing a few early blossoms that drifted down around them like soft snow.

When he finally spoke,  his voice was low, rough,  like it hurt to force the words out.

**JEFF:** (quiet, controlled)  
I *knew*.

Jen leaned closer, listening, not daring to interrupt.

**JEFF:** (flat, tight)  
I knew the *second* they looked at you.  The second they *shifted* you out of the corridor.  I knew what they were *thinking*.  What they were *doing*.

He closed his eyes for a beat, breathed through his nose, controlled the storm.

**JEFF:** (even)  
And I had two choices.  I could stay.  Smile.  Pretend like you weren’t standing right there —  pretend like my *wife* didn’t just get shoved aside like she was some mistake —  so I could close a deal.  *Make* some money.  *Win* some favor.

His hands tightened into fists on his thighs, knuckles whitening.

**JEFF:** (quiet, iron-hard)  
Or I could walk.

He opened his eyes —  and they were blazing.

**JEFF:** (low, fierce)  
So I walked.  Because no amount of money…  no contract, no album, no market…  is worth the price of disrespecting you.

Jen pressed a hand to her mouth, holding back a sob. Jeff kept going, his voice still so quiet it barely carried —  but every word fell like a hammer.

**JEFF:** (raw, steady)  
You’re my wife.  You’re not baggage.  You’re not a mistake.  You’re not a liability.

He turned on the bench, fully facing her now —  his expression naked, burning with furious devotion.

**JEFF:** (softer, hoarse)  
You are my helpmate.  My partner.  Bone of my bone, flesh of my flesh.

A blossom caught in Jen’s hair, and Jeff reached up with trembling fingers to brush it away —  gentle, reverent.

**JEFF:** (whispering)  
I would rather lose the whole world  than let anyone treat you like you’re anything less than my other half.

The words hung there —  fragile, crystalline, eternal.

Jen couldn’t stop the tears now, even if she wanted to. She surged forward, wrapping her arms around his neck,  pressing her face into his flannel shirt,  feeling the trembling in his chest where he finally, finally let go of the control.

He crushed her to him —  holding her like he never intended to let go —  burying his face in her hair. And together, under the blooming cherry trees,  in the middle of a city of forty million strangers,  they built a small, indestructible fortress of love and honor and stubborn, unstoppable faith.

---

They stayed wrapped up in each other for a long minute,  the world narrowing down to just the two of them and the slow, steady thud of their hearts.

Finally, Jen pulled back slightly,  wiping at her eyes with the heel of her hand,  sniffling out a watery little laugh.

**JEN:** (soft, tentative)  
So…    What's the plan, husband?

Jeff leaned back, scrubbing a hand down his face.  The tension was still there,  but it had settled now —  heavy but manageable, not a firestorm.  He glanced around, taking in the glossy buildings, the rivers of fast-moving suits, the glint of sunlight off chrome and glass. His gaze caught something across the street, bright, bold, unmistakable.

The Golden Arches.

McDonald's. A sliver of America planted right in the heart of Tokyo.  Jeff exhaled slowly,  his mouth quirking into something that wasn’t quite a smile —  but wasn’t bitterness either.

**JEFF:** (quiet, decisive)  
Right now?  I want something American.  I want to sit under those Golden Arches,  eat something fried and greasy,  and remember who I am.

Jen turned to follow his gaze and immediately burst out laughing —  a warm, genuine sound that chased away the last of the sorrow.

**JEN:** (grinning)  
You want a Big Mac to go with your righteous fury?

Jeff finally gave her a real smile — tired, but true.

**JEFF:** (teasing)  
Yeah.  And maybe some fries.  Super-sized.

Jen slipped her hand into his again — their connection feeling stronger, cleaner somehow, after everything that had just passed.

**JEN:** (sweet, sure)  
Then let’s go be Americans.  Full throttle.

Jeff chuckled under his breath, stood up, and tugged her to her feet.

Together they crossed the street, hand in hand,  two tiny dots of stubborn love and battered dignity,  headed for the bright yellow sanctuary of American cheese, salty fries, and something that, for the first time all morning, felt like home.

---

The inside of the McDonald’s felt… strange.  Familiar but off.  Like stepping into a dream of America that someone else had built from secondhand photos.  The walls were cleaner.  The tables were smaller.  The staff bowed politely at the counter.  The overhead menu was bright and full of familiar colors —  but the words…the words were different.  Jen leaned in, squinting at the menu overhead.

**JEN:** (grinning)  
You know…some of this looks familiar…but they’ve got some weird stuff on the menu here too.

She pointed at something with brightly colored pickles stacked like bricks.

Jeff laughed under his breath, feeling some of the last tension bleed out of his spine.

**JEFF:** (dry)  
Welcome to international fast food, baby.

He peered up at the board, studying it like a man approaching a battlefield.

**JEFF:** (muttering)  
Never really stopped at a McDonald's here before…I wonder if the Big Mac tastes the same.  Wonder if the fries hold up.  Wonder if—

He stopped, grimaced slightly.

**JEFF:** (mock tragic)  
They're not gonna have Mountain Dew, are they.

Jen stifled a giggle behind her hand, watching his genuine, almost boyish dismay.

**JEN:** (teasing)  
Nope. Coke or bust.

Jeff made a face — the same one she imagined he made when forced to listen to bad demo tapes —  
then sighed, theatrical and martyred.

**JEFF:** (resigned)  
I’ll settle for Coke.

He turned to her —  eyes serious, voice mock solemn.

**JEFF:** (softly, playful)  
But only for you.

Jen beamed at him —  
warm, fierce, proud —  
and reached up to push a stray lock of hair off his forehead.

**JEN:** (sweet, laughing)  
You’re a true patriot, Mr. Cremeans.

They stepped forward together, hand in hand,  
ready to order —  
ready to reclaim something simple and joyful in a morning that had started in fire.

Jeff squinted one last time at the menu.

**JEFF:** (deadpan)  
If that purple teriyaki burger kills me,  
  
bury me under a cherry tree.  
  
Tell them I died with honor.

Jen snorted out a laugh so loud it drew polite, startled glances from the staff —  
and for the first time all day,  
Jeff felt the last tight band around his heart finally start to loosen.


---

They placed their orders —  
Jen, impulsively mischievous, pointing confidently at the purple teriyaki burger;  
Jeff, sticking with tradition:  
Big Mac, supersized fries, Coke.

The trays slid toward them,  
and Jeff carried them to a table by the window with a stiff, mock-military posture,  
like a man protecting sacred cargo.

They sat down —  
finally, finally —  
and the world outside kept spinning,  
but here, in this silly, bright corner of Tokyo,  
they carved out a moment just for themselves.

Jeff unwrapped his Big Mac with the reverence of a man opening a priceless artifact.  
Jen popped the lid off her Coke and grinned at him over the rim.

He raised the burger,  
took a massive bite,  
chewed slowly.

And then —  
with a look of stunned, almost childlike awe —  
he said:

**JEFF:** (mouth half-full, wonderstruck)  
This tastes just like Ohio.  
  
Exactly like Ohio.

Jen snorted her Coke up her nose trying not to laugh.

She set the cup down, dabbing her mouth with a napkin, grinning like crazy.

**JEN:** (laughing)  
Should I be concerned that you can recognize your home state by flavor?

Jeff finished chewing, swallowed, and gave a dramatic, fake-serious nod.

**JEFF:** (mock formal)  
When you've eaten as many drive-thru meals between Cincinnati and Columbus as I have, you develop a refined palate.

Jen shook her head, giggling, and unwrapped her own burger.  The teriyaki glaze shimmered under the fluorescent lights —  
sticky, deep purple, dangerous looking.

She eyed it warily.

**JEN:** (mock brave)  
If I die…Tell my parents I loved them.  And I died for the cause.

Jeff leaned back in his seat, grinning at her, his whole body relaxing now —  the tight coil inside him slowly, finally unwinding.

He watched as Jen took a tentative bite.

She froze, chewing thoughtfully.

**JEN:** (surprised)  
…That's actually really good.

Jeff laughed, genuinely this time —  the sound warm, rolling, rich.

**JEFF:** (grinning)  
See?  Stick with me, Mrs. Cremeans.  I’ll show you the world.

Jen reached across the tiny plastic table and grabbed his hand again, squeezing it —  silent, fierce, grateful.

And for the first time all day, everything felt simple again.  Just food.  Just laughter.  Just them.  Exactly how it should be.

---

They lingered over the food, savoring every bite —  not rushing, not thinking about the outside world.  Just being.  Jen picked up a single fry, held it up between two fingers, and waved it in front of Jeff’s face like a prize.

**JEN:** (teasing)  
You gonna be brave, Mr. Cremeans?  You gonna trust Tokyo French fries?

Jeff leaned forward dramatically, hands clasped behind his back, like a kid at a carnival game.

**JEFF:** (mock solemn)  
I have absolute faith…in American potato technology.

Jen laughed and popped the fry into his mouth.  He bit down, chewed thoughtfully.  And then his eyes widened in theatrical surprise.

**JEFF:** (muffled)  
Holy smokes.

Jen's eyebrows shot up.

**JEN:** (grinning)  
Good?

Jeff nodded furiously, reaching for a fry of his own.  He grabbed one — golden, crisp, practically glowing —  and offered it to her with an exaggerated flourish.

**JEFF:** (grinning)  
Your turn, Mrs. Cremeans.  Show some faith.

Jen leaned forward, eyes sparkling, and accepted the offering.  She bit down — and immediately made a soft, delighted sound.

**JEN:** (chewing, impressed)  
Oh man.  Golden brown, salty, crisp on the outside…fluffy on the inside…

She shook her head in wonder.

**JEN:** (mock serious)  
These are better than half the fries in Ohio.

Jeff placed a hand over his heart.

**JEFF:** (grinning)  
High praise coming from a Midwestern girl.

They fed each other fries back and forth, laughing softly, sometimes missing and dropping them, sometimes getting it perfect —  and somehow, somehow, this tiny silly thing felt like the best part of the day.

Because it wasn’t about fries.  It wasn’t even about Tokyo anymore.  It was about sitting across from the one person who mattered, after a morning that could have broken them, and realizing that they were still whole.  Still together.  Still choosing each other —  over everything.

---

The tea pavilion was simple —  
a handful of low lacquered tables,  
smooth tatami mats underfoot,  
the scent of cherry blossoms drifting in on the cool breeze.

A slender woman in a pale green kimono approached,  
kneeling gracefully on the mat before them.  
Her movements were deliberate, precise —  
like a dance.

Jeff and Jen settled onto cushions,  
awkwardly at first —  
their American bodies not used to folding so neatly onto the floor —  
but managing with quiet smiles.

No one spoke.

The woman bowed deeply, hands flat on the mat before her,

trying to mirror her respect.

She moved through the ritual without hurry,

the fine ceramic bowls,  
the bamboo whisk,  
the tiny lacquered caddy of powdered green tea.

**JEN:** (whispering)

As they floated lazily in the hot spring,

this unashamed —  
  
with so many strangers around…

She shook her head slowly, marveling at herself.

**JEN:** (smiling)  
It’s a change of pace.  
  
A good one.

Jeff’s heart squeezed in his chest —  
and he reached under the water to find her hand,  
twining their fingers together beneath the surface.

**JEFF:** (soft, reverent)  
You're beautiful.  
  
And you're mine.

Jen smiled at him —  
bright and wicked and sweet all at once.

**JEN:** (teasing)  
Darn right I am.

And together they floated —  
weightless, fearless, alive —  
in a moment that would never come again,  
but would live forever between them.


---

Jen leaned back against the smooth stone edge of the bath,  
her fingers still laced with Jeff’s under the hot water,  
her eyes half-lidded in perfect, melting peace.

And then —  
out of nowhere —  
her voice broke the comfortable silence.

**JEN:** (quiet, thoughtful)  
When we get back to the States…  
  
I'm going to have to write this down.

Jeff turned his head toward her, curious.

**JEFF:** (softly)  
Write it down?

Jen nodded slowly, a dreamy smile playing at her lips.

**JEN:** (gently)  
All of it.  
  
Start to finish.  
  
Tahiti.  
  
Tokyo.  
  
The boat, the beaches, the subway, the baths, this.

She gestured vaguely at the mist swirling around them.

**JEN:** (earnest, glowing)  
I don't know if I'll ever show it to anyone.  
  
Maybe not even our kids.

She laughed softly —  
a self-conscious sound.

**JEN:** (musing)  
But…  
  
if I don't take the time to really capture it —  
how it feels right now,  
how alive everything is,  
how big everything feels —  
I'll lose pieces of it.  
  
It’ll get fuzzy around the edges.

Jeff squeezed her hand under the water —  
solid, real, here.

**JEFF:** (low, certain)  
Then write it down, babe.

Jen tilted her head toward him —  
grateful, luminous.

**JEN:** (softly)  
Maybe one day…  
  
when our little girls or little boys are grown…  
  
they'll want to know who we were.  
  
Before we were just "Mom" and "Dad."

Jeff smiled —  
and there was something proud, something deeply moved,  
something utterly in love  
in the way he looked at her then.

**JEFF:** (teasing gently)  
If you write it half as good as you live it,  
  
they're gonna think we were rock stars.

Jen giggled, wrinkling her nose.

**JEN:** (grinning)  
Well…  
  
you do manage a band.

Jeff laughed out loud —  
startling a few lazy ripples across the bath’s surface —  
and leaned in to kiss her lightly on the forehead.

**JEFF:** (whispered)  
You’re my greatest adventure, Jen Cremeans.  
  
Write it all down.

And she smiled —  
bright and sure —  
because she would.

She would.


---

They were drying off now, side by side,  
toweling off the last lingering heat of the bath,  
wrapping themselves back into their yukata.

Jen turned toward him —  
her hair damp, her cheeks flushed —  
and asked the question that had been simmering in the back of her mind all morning.

**JEN:** (quiet, careful)  
What are you going to do about Sony?

Jeff paused —  just for a second —  snapping gears instantly, his whole bearing shifting into something colder, harder,  something Jen had only glimpsed in flashes before.

He tugged the sash of his robe tight,  his voice low, flat, deliberate.

**JEFF:** (calm, final)  
I'm going to let them rot.

Jen blinked —  startled, but not frightened.

Jeff straightened, his eyes distant now —  already thinking three moves ahead.

**JEFF:** (cold)  
I'm going to let them disintegrate.  Turn to dust and ash.

He glanced at her,  making sure she was hearing him clearly.

**JEFF:** (quiet steel)  
I'm certain Sarah Malcolm's read my message by now.  And frankly… I don't give a damn about Sony Music Japan.

Jen held his gaze —  refused to recoil at the calm, almost clinical finality in his voice. 
Because this wasn’t cruelty.  This wasn’t rage.  This was justice. This was respect. This was Jeff Cremeans —  a man who loved clean, who loved right,  and who would burn the world down if anyone dared tarnish what he held dear.

Jen moved closer —  rested her forehead lightly against his shoulder.

**JEN:** (whispering)  
I’m proud of you.

Jeff exhaled slowly —  some of the hard tension leaking out of him.

He pressed a kiss into her damp hair,  his arms wrapping around her instinctively.

**JEFF:** (soft, fierce)  
You’re not optional.  They forgot that.

And she knew —  deep in her bones —  that no matter where life took them,  no matter how bright or dark the road ahead,  he would never, ever forget.


---

They stepped out of the bath slowly —  bodies dripping, skin flushed —  but the emotional heat between them was different now.

Jen dried herself off with small, deliberate motions,  
watching Jeff out of the corner of her eye  
as he pulled his robe back on, tied the sash,  
his movements efficient, clean.

There was laughter —  
there was lightness —  
about the last lingering bits of modesty as they dressed again.  
She teased him gently about how nonchalantly he flopped around,  
and he grinned, playful, easy.

But underneath it —  
underneath it all —  
Jen felt the weight of what she had seen.

The other nakedness.  
The emotional nakedness.

Not just Jeff’s body,  
but Jeff’s soul laid bare —  
in that cold, decisive, steel-edged moment where he defended her  
without raising his voice,  
without losing control.

It was beautiful.  
It was terrifying.  
It was awe-inspiring.

As she slipped her arms into the sleeves of her robe,  
she glanced at him —  
and found her voice.

**JEN:** (soft, serious)  
Are you going to act like that…  
  
toward our kids?  
  
When you need to discipline them?

Jeff paused, his hands still for a moment on the sash he was tying.

He turned and looked at her —  
really looked at her —  
his face open, thoughtful.

**JEFF:** (quiet)  
I hope not all the time.

He took a step toward her —  
careful, deliberate —  
closing the space between them.

**JEFF:** (gently)  
But if I ever have to…  
  
if I ever have to draw a hard line,  
  
if I have to make something crystal clear…  
  
then, yeah.  
  
They’ll know exactly where I stand.

Jen swallowed hard,  
feeling the quiet promise in his voice.

He reached out and tucked a damp strand of hair behind her ear,  
his touch achingly tender.

**JEFF:** (smiling faintly)  
But most days?  
  
Most days they'll get the guy who makes terrible dad jokes.  
  
The guy who sneaks ice cream into the living room.  
  
The guy who builds blanket forts.

Jen smiled —  
a full, radiant thing —  
and leaned into his touch.

**JEN:** (whispering)  
I think they’re going to love you, Jeff Cremeans.

He laughed softly —  
a low, humble sound —  
and leaned in to kiss her forehead.

**JEFF:** (smiling)  
As long as they love you more.

Jen laughed —  
the pure sound of it echoing off the quiet wooden walls —  
and together, still damp, still glowing,  
still changed,  
they walked side-by-side toward the breakfast hall,  
toward whatever came next.


---

They walked together down the quiet, polished hallways of the ryokan,  
their sandals whispering on the tatami mats,  
their yukata rustling softly with every step.

Jen glanced up at her husband,  
still pink from the bath,  
still glowing from the conversation they'd shared.

**JEN:** (half-laughing, half-wondering)  
So…  
  
what's for breakfast?

Jeff chuckled —  
that easy, warm sound she was learning to treasure.

**JEFF:** (grinning)  
Traditional Japanese breakfast.  
  
Whatever that means.

Jen wrinkled her nose playfully.

**JEN:** (teasing)  
You don't know either, do you?

Jeff threw his hands up in mock surrender.

**JEFF:** (laughing)  
Nope.  
  
We're gonna find out together.

They slid open the paper doors to the breakfast room —  
and immediately, Jen froze, wide-eyed.

Small, low wooden tables.  
Cushions instead of chairs.  
Neatly arranged lacquer trays at each place setting,  
each one a tiny world of color and texture.

Jeff smiled at her reaction —  
then led her gently forward.

They knelt side-by-side, tucking their legs carefully,  
and a quiet, bowing attendant brought their trays.

Jen leaned close to examine hers.

Small grilled fish.  
A bowl of white rice.  
A little dish of pickled vegetables —  
bright pink and deep green.  
A tiny square of tamagoyaki — sweet rolled omelet.  
Miso soup, steaming gently.  
And a perfect little portion of nori — dried seaweed.

Jen blinked —  
stunned by the meticulous beauty of it all.

**JEN:** (whispering)  
It's like…  
  
it's like food made for dolls.

Jeff laughed under his breath.

**JEFF:** (grinning)  
Yeah.  
  
Presentation is not American.

He picked up his chopsticks carefully,  
testing the weight in his hand.

**JEFF:** (teasing)  
Now watch me make a fool of myself trying to eat this.

Jen picked up hers too —  
clumsily —  
and then they both dissolved into quiet, giddy laughter  
at the small, fumbling adventure of it all.

Because this was what they had promised each other:  
not just sex,  
not just marriage,  
not just a house and babies and Sunday dinners —  
but life.

The whole, messy, beautiful, unfamiliar, astonishing world.

And they were going to live it all —  
together.


---

Jeff poked a suspicious-looking pickled vegetable with his chopsticks,  
eyebrow raised.

**JEFF:** (wry)  
You know, I'm a meat and potatoes guy, Jen.  
  
Pickled vegetables for breakfast is a bit much.

Jen giggled softly,  
grinning as she tried to tackle the grilled fish with far more enthusiasm than skill.

**JEN:** (teasing)  
Grilled fish for breakfast?  
  
I prefer bacon and sausage.  
  
Ham if I get ambitious.

Jeff smirked at her —  
his eyes dancing with amusement.

**JEFF:** (grinning)  
Now that sounds like a breakfast I understand.

Jen leaned a little closer, whispering conspiratorially.

**JEN:** (grinning)  
But…  
  
hey.  
  
If you present it like this —  
all fancy and delicate and beautiful —  
you can get away with anything.

She swept her hand dramatically over the tray,  
mocking the elegance of it all.

**JEN:** (playful)  
Japan’s great.

Jeff nodded solemnly, picking up his bowl of miso soup like it was a sacred artifact.

**JEFF:** (mock serious)  
We are on Mars.

Jen covered her mouth to stifle a giggle.

**JEN:** (laughing softly)  
This is an alien culture.  
  
And I absolutely adore it.

Jeff smiled —  
wide, easy, full of pride.

**JEFF:** (warmly)  
Me too.

They clinked their miso bowls together in a silent toast —  
two earthlings lost somewhere far from home,  
absolutely loving every strange, beautiful second.

---

After breakfast,  with the morning sun just starting to filter through the misty green hills,  they wandered hand-in-hand down the narrow stone paths leading into the nearby country village.

It was like stepping into another world —  smaller, simpler, quieter.

Tiny shops with hand-painted signs.  Ancient shrines tucked into corners,  their wood worn smooth by centuries of hands.  The occasional old woman tending a flower bed,  
bowing with a warm smile as they passed.

Jeff and Jen moved slowly —  without a plan,  without a hurry.

They peered into little markets,  where fresh vegetables gleamed in baskets:  bright green okra, round fat daikon, delicate bundles of shiso leaves.  Farmers bowed and offered them samples,  and Jeff, laughing, accepted a sliver of pickled radish so sour it made him visibly flinch.

**JEFF:** (grinning)  
That’s a little more fight than I expected at ten in the morning.

Jen giggled —  the sound light and clear in the cool air.

**JEN:** (smirking)  
You’re tough.  You survived my hand job at dawn.  You can survive a radish.

Jeff gave her a mock glare, but he was smiling.

As they rounded another quiet bend,  Jeff spotted something small and familiar tucked behind a stone wall.  A simple white steeple. He squinted at the faded wooden sign —  
written in both Japanese and charmingly broken English.

**JEFF:** (soft, smiling)  
I think that's a Baptist church.

Jen shaded her eyes with her hand, looking closer.

They stepped up to the tiny gate —  and realized, no. The small plaque underneath clarified:  Methodist Mission, Est. 1897.  Jeff huffed a little.

**JEFF:** (wry)  
Close enough for government work.

Jen looped her arm through his, smiling up at him.

**JEN:** (softly)  
It’ll do.

They stood there for a moment —  listening to the cicadas buzzing in the distance,  
the rustle of the fields,  the hush of the wind through ancient trees.

The same God, halfway across the world.  The same heartbeat between them.  Jeff squeezed her arm gently.

**JEFF:** (whispering)  
You and me, babe.  Anywhere on this earth.

Jen smiled —  bright and sure —  and leaned her head against his shoulder.

**JEN:** (whispering)  
Anywhere.

And then they kept walking,  hand in hand,  two little specks of light moving through a bright, beautiful world.


---

By a little past one o'clock in the afternoon,  their stomachs started reminding them —  politely at first, then more insistently —  that breakfast had been hours ago.

But neither of them felt ready to leave the sleepy charm of the village. So, when they stumbled across a tiny wooden café,  tucked between a field of sunflowers and a small, crumbling stone bridge,  they exchanged a quick, knowing look.

**JEFF:** (grinning)  
Adventure?

**JEN:** (grinning wider)  
Adventure.

They pushed open the sliding door —  a little bell tinkled —  and stepped inside.

It was tiny.  Maybe five tables total.  Bright paper lanterns hung from the low ceiling.  
The smell of rice and roasting meat was so good it almost knocked them over.

A woman — middle-aged, apron-draped, smiling wide —  bounded over toward them.  She said something —  something long, melodic, utterly incomprehensible —  
and bowed deeply.

Jeff bowed back, awkward but sincere.  Jen followed a half-beat later, biting her lip to keep from laughing. And that's when the first problem became crystal clear:  no one spoke a lick of English.  Other than maybe "please" and "thank you" —  the Cremeans were hopeless.  Still, they were determined.

Jeff mimed eating with an invisible bowl and chopsticks.  Jen clapped her hands together like she was about to pray — the universal gesture for "delicious." The woman roared with laughter —  a big, bold, belly laugh that echoed through the entire café.  Jen giggled too, covering her mouth.

**JEN:** (whispering to Jeff)  
I think she likes us.

**JEFF:** (whispering back)  
I think she thinks we're complete idiots.

He grinned at the woman —  gave an exaggerated thumbs-up —  and she gave two back, her whole face lighting up.  Somehow, with grins and gestures and bursts of pantomime,  they managed to order… something.  Exactly what, they had no idea.  They took their seats at a small wooden table in the corner,  elbows almost bumping because the space was so tight.

**JEN:** (giggling)  
I hope it's not pickled octopus.

**JEFF:** (mock grimacing)  
I’ll eat it if you eat it.

**JEN:** (smirking)  
Deal.

They laughed, leaning back in their chairs,  utterly at ease, utterly alive. Because this —  
this moment of foolishness, of clumsiness, of wide-eyed wonder —  this was everything they had promised each other. And when the food came —  steaming, fragrant, beautiful, they dug in with genuine joy, no matter what strange mysteries they were about to taste.


---

The server returned, still chuckling softly to herself,  carrying two deep, lacquered bowls,  each one crowned with a perfect swirl of steam. She set them down with a flourish —  a little bow, a cheerful chirp of something that might have been "enjoy!" —  and bustled away, still grinning.

Jeff leaned in first, cautiously.  Jen mirrored him —  then they both inhaled at the same moment. 
And froze.

**JEN:** (brightly astonished)  
Beef.  It’s beef!

Jeff grinned wide, almost in disbelief.

**JEFF:** (grinning)  
Or…  what the Japanese serve when they’re serving beef.

The smell was incredible —  rich, savory, familiar and foreign all at once.  A dark, sweet soy glaze clung to tender strips of marinated beef, nestled atop a generous bed of steaming white rice.  Tiny flecks of green onion floated like confetti over the top,  along with a scattering of sesame seeds.

Jeff picked up his chopsticks —  
a little more confident this time —  
and snagged a piece, blowing on it once before popping it into his mouth.

He closed his eyes in bliss.

**JEFF:** (muttering with his mouth half full)  
Oh my God.

Jen tried a bite herself — and immediately her face lit up.

**JEN:** (delighted)  
It's like… like Midwestern pot roast and teriyaki had a beautiful baby!

Jeff chuckled, reaching for his tea.

**JEFF:** (grinning)  
This right here?  This is why you travel.

They ate slowly, savoring every bite,  laughing over their bad chopstick skills,  taking tiny breaks just to grin at each other across the table.

Because somehow —  in this tiny café, halfway across the world —  they had found a perfect moment:  familiar,  foreign,  funny,  theirs. And it smelled like home.

---

They were sitting back,  stuffed, happy, utterly satisfied —  when the owner herself came bustling back toward them,  wiping her hands on her apron, a mischievous twinkle in her eyes. In her hands was a small, delicate dish —  no bigger than a saucer —  holding a tiny, jewel-like dessert.

She set it down between them with a proud little bow,  chattering away in Japanese they couldn't hope to understand,  then stood back with her hands clasped,  beaming like a grandmother watching grandkids open Christmas presents.

Jeff looked at Jen. Jen looked at Jeff. Both of them looked at the tiny, beautiful thing.  It was…what on earth was it?  A small, perfectly formed ball —  translucent, almost glassy —  resting on a tiny green leaf.  Beside it, a drizzle of dark syrup and a small pile of what looked like ground soybean powder.

**JEN:** (whispering)  
Is it…Is it a pearl?  Are we supposed to eat that?

Jeff leaned in, squinting.

**JEFF:** (grinning)  
If it’s jewelry, it's really fresh.

They looked up at the owner — who was smiling so widely now that she was almost vibrating with anticipation.

Jen picked up her chopsticks carefully, scooped a little of the syrup onto the shimmering orb, and popped it into her mouth. For a second she sat absolutely still —  then her face lit up.

**JEN:** (muffled)  
It’s like…a raindrop!  A sweet, melty, magical raindrop!

Jeff laughed, delighted,  and tried his bite too.  It was light —  barely sweet —  with a subtle earthy note from the powder and a little punch from the syrup.

**JEFF:** (grinning)  
It's like candy made out of rain and sunshine.

The owner laughed —  a deep, happy sound —  and clapped her hands once, pleased beyond measure.  Jen and Jeff bowed awkwardly and exaggeratedly —  causing another big burst of laughter from the owner —  and then sat back, grinning like fools.

**JEN:** (softly)  
I love this country.

**JEFF:** (grinning)  
I love you.

Jen smiled —  slow and sure —  and reached across the table to squeeze his hand.

Outside, the cicadas droned lazily,  the fields stretched golden under the afternoon sun,  
and the whole wide world felt astonishingly, impossibly good.

---

Full, content, hand in hand,  
Jeff and Jen wandered slowly back toward the ryokan.

The little country road twisted through rice paddies and low stone walls,  
past rows of bowed sunflowers,  
past old men napping outside shops,  
past children chasing each other with paper fans.

They moved in a soft, easy silence —  
each step a quiet promise to savor every last breath of this day.

And then, just as they crested a small hill —  
there it was.

Snuck right in the middle of nowhere —  
modest, almost hidden behind a curtain of green trees —  
stood a tiny whitewashed building.

A simple wooden sign swayed gently in the breeze:

First Baptist Mission of Nagano County  
Founded 1952

Jen gasped, squeezing Jeff’s hand.

**JEN:** (whispering, almost reverent)  
There it is.  
  
There it is.

Jeff stared, a slow grin blooming across his face.

**JEFF:** (softly)  
Told you.  
  
God plants little seeds in all kinds of soil.

The building was small —  
no bigger than a two-room schoolhouse —  
with a humble steeple, a neat row of wooden benches outside,  
and what looked like a cracked but still-useful bell hanging proudly from a low frame.

There was no service today —  
just quiet.  
Just the cicadas.  
Just the distant hum of the world.

They stood there for a moment —  
still as statues —  
feeling the unexpected rightness of it all.

**JEN:** (soft)  
I wish we had service clothes on.  
  
I'd walk right in.

**JEFF:** (smiling)  
We can still pray.

He dropped to a knee without hesitation —  
pulling her gently down with him —  
and together, right there on the side of the road,  
halfway around the world,  
under a soft blue sky,  
they bowed their heads.

**JEFF:** (quiet prayer)  
Thank you, Father,  
for this day,  
for this place,  
for the woman beside me,  
for the life we’re building.

**JEN:** (softly adding)  
And thank you for new mornings,  
new mercies,  
and for finding you…  
no matter where we are.

They stayed like that a moment longer —  
two tiny figures lost in the endless beauty of the world.

And then Jeff stood, offering her his hand.

**JEFF:** (smiling)  
Come on, Mrs. Cremeans.  
  
We’ve still got a whole evening left to live.

Jen smiled up at him, took his hand —  
and together, they walked back into the golden afternoon.


---

They returned to the ryokan just as the sun was beginning to tilt low,  
the light stretching long and golden across the polished wood floors.

There was a traditional tea service waiting —  
a final, formal farewell before their departure.

They knelt at the low table,  
accepting the small, delicate cups with both hands,  
bowing politely to their host,  
smiling warmly as best they could despite the quiet ache of departure.

The tea was soft, grassy, and faintly sweet —  
like drinking a memory.  
They sipped it slowly,  
reluctantly,  
trying to stretch the moment just a little longer.

When the time came,  
they gathered their few belongings,  
bowed deeply to the staff —  
who bowed even deeper in return —  
and stepped out into the warm afternoon air.

The train station was a short walk away.  
The bullet train waited for them — sleek, silent, ready to hurtle them back toward the neon blaze of Tokyo.

As they boarded and found their seats,  
Jeff slouched back into the cushioned chair,  
running a hand through his hair,  
still a little rumpled from the bath.

**JEFF:** (grinning, tired)  
You know…  
  
I don't know what to do tomorrow.

Jen chuckled, tucking her legs up underneath her,  
sitting sideways to better watch him.

**JEN:** (teasing)  
That’s a lot of half-a-days you had scheduled, Mr. Cremeans.  
  
Half a day socializing.  
Half a day of business.  
Half a day at the ballpark.

Jeff laughed —  
a low, worn-out sound.

**JEFF:** (mock serious)  
That's because there's a lot of stuff that overlaps, Mrs. Cremeans.  
  
Very complicated stuff.

Jen raised an eyebrow.

**JEN:** (grinning)  
Oh, very official.

Jeff turned to face her fully now,  
his blue eyes glinting with mischief.

**JEFF:** (casual)  
I don't know.  
  
Maybe we just stay in bed all day.  
  
Have sex all day.

Jen burst into laughter —  
the sound light, sweet, and full of love.

**JEN:** (mock scandalized)  
Sir!  
  
I’m a married woman!

**JEFF:** (grinning)  
Exactly.

They grinned at each other —  
two conspirators,  
two best friends,  
two lovers hurtling at two hundred miles an hour back toward the waiting world.

Outside the windows,  
the countryside blurred past in soft streaks of green and gold,  
but inside their little bubble,  
time moved slow,  
easy,  
forever.


---

The train ride seemed like it took no time at all.

The scenery outside — the rice fields, the rivers, the distant mountains —  
flashed past in a dreamy blur.

But inside their little world,  
time slowed and stretched in the most wonderful way.

Jen stretched lazily in her seat,  
twisting just enough to face Jeff more fully.

**JEN:** (grinning)  
Okay, mister big ideas.  
  
Staying in bed all day sounds fun, but what exactly is on the menu?

Jeff chuckled, low and warm.

**JEFF:** (teasing)  
Depends.  
  
What do you want to be on the menu?

Jen narrowed her eyes playfully.

**JEN:** (mock stern)  
I'm serious.  
  
You're the one who promised the day of all days.

Jeff leaned closer, dropping his voice a little, just for her.

**JEFF:** (soft)  
What can I do for you?  
  
You're the one that has the 16-point playbook.  
  
How many have we scratched off so far?  
  
Five?

Jen gave him a sly, wicked grin.

**JEN:** (smirking)  
Six.

Jeff whistled low.

**JEFF:** (grinning)  
Overachievers.

**JEN:** (giggling)  
Speak for yourself, champ.

Jeff laughed and slouched back in his seat,  
drumming his fingers lightly against his thigh.

**JEFF:** (thoughtfully)  
Alright.  
  
We can start the day slow.  
  
Breakfast in bed…  
maybe just coffee…  
some lazy morning kisses…  
  
And then maybe you pick a number off that list.

Jen raised an eyebrow.

**JEN:** (teasing)  
And if I pick two numbers?

Jeff flashed her that slow, crooked grin that always made her heart skip.

**JEFF:** (mock serious)  
Then it’s gonna be a long day, Mrs. Cremeans.

She laughed —  
bright, light, alive.

And Jeff knew, as surely as he knew anything in this world,  
that this woman —  
this incredible, complicated, fascinating woman —  
was the greatest adventure he would ever have.

Outside, the signs of Tokyo began to thicken —  
taller buildings, wider roads, a faster pulse.

But inside their bubble,  
it was still just the two of them,  
writing the next chapter,  
one heartbeat at a time.

---

Dragging their small suitcases behind them,  
Jeff and Jen stepped into the warm Tokyo evening.

The city pulsed and hummed around them —  
not the frantic, roaring energy of rush hour,  
but a softer, glowing rhythm:  
neon signs buzzing overhead,  
shopkeepers pulling down shutters,  
couples laughing quietly as they ducked into tiny restaurants.

It felt different.  
Easier.  
More human.

They made their way down the narrow stairs into the subway station,  
the clack-clack of their luggage wheels echoing off the concrete walls.

Down below, it was busy —  
still Tokyo, after all —  
but it wasn’t suffocating anymore.

There were people, sure —  
men in suits, teenagers in uniforms, older women carrying shopping bags —  
but the sheer density had eased.

They could breathe.  
They could move.

They found a spot near one of the poles,  
and for once, they didn’t have to brace themselves against a tide of humanity.  
They stood side-by-side, bodies almost brushing,  
but this time not out of necessity —  
out of choice.
